Classification: Manipulated

Identified Attack:
- Most Likely: Attack 0
- Confidence: High
- Reasoning: The query day contains 96 consecutive zero readings, whereas the reference week shows consistent non-zero consumption with daily minima ranging from 19.78 to 27.44. A full-day zero profile is physically implausible and clearly manipulated. Attack 0 explicitly describes replacing all samples with zero for the entire observation period, matching the observed pattern exactly. Alternative Attack 2 (on‑off) would zero only a random duration, and Attack IEEE would zero only some intervals—neither would produce a full day of uninterrupted zeros. Hence, Attack 0 is the only plausible choice.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: Every 15‑minute reading is forced to 0.0, completely removing any trace of the typical daily load curve visible in the reference week (e.g., morning ramp, midday peak, evening decline).  
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Magnitude is reduced to zero, and the trend is entirely eliminated, yielding a flat, constant zero profile.  
- Possible technical causes: A total meter bypass (physical or magnetic) that prevents any measurement from being recorded, a severe meter malfunction, or a full‑scale false data injection that commands the meter to report permanent zero usage.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Output Format:
Classification:
- Original | Manipulated

Identified Attack:  (fill only if manipulated)
- Most Likely: Attack X
- Confidence: High | Medium | Low
- Reasoning: Why it matches Attack X and why Attack Y and Attack Z were ruled out (list top 2 alternatives considered)

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:  (fill only if manipulated)
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week
- Impact on magnitude and trend
- Possible technical causes
Reasoning content 
We are given a reference week of energy consumption for a smart meter, and a query day. The query day data shows all 96 readings as 0.00. This immediately indicates an attack where all consumption samples are replaced with zero values. This matches Attack 0 exactly: "Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration."

We must confirm: Is it manipulated? Yes, because the query day has zero consumption throughout, which is inconsistent with the normal week. The reference week shows typical residential consumption patterns with daily cycles and nonzero minimums (lowest min in reference week is 19.78 on Day 1; all days have min > 0). So this is clearly manipulated.

Attack type: Attack 0. Confidence: High, because the entire day is exactly zeros, matching the precise definition.

Alternatives: Attack 2 (on-off attack with zeros for a random duration) could also produce zeros, but here it's the entire day, not just a random duration. Attack IEEE also zeroes some intervals, but not all. So Attack 0 is the best match.

Now, output format:

Classification: Manipulated

Identified Attack:
- Most Likely: Attack 0
- Confidence: High
- Reasoning: The query day shows a flat zero consumption for all 96 readings. In the reference week, every day has a clear pattern with nonzero minimum values (lowest daily min is 19.78). A full day of zero usage is physically unrealistic and indicates manipulation. Attack 0 is defined as replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration, which exactly describes this scenario. Attack 2 (on-off attack) would zero out only a random duration, not the entire day. Attack IEEE would zero only some intervals, leaving others as actual. Thus, Attack 0 is the most likely.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: Instead of the normal fluctuating consumption pattern, all 15-minute interval readings are reported as 0.0. This completely eliminates any indication of energy usage.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Magnitude is reduced to zero; trend is completely removed, resulting in a flat line.
- Possible technical causes: Total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction, where no energy usage is recorded at all.

Output accordingly.

Reasoning content 
We are given a reference week of energy consumption for a smart meter (CONS_NO=16, May, Week 2, various days) and a query day (DAY_ID=3 in June, Wednesday 2012-06-06). The reference week days show typical consumption patterns with min values around 3.5-5.5, max 12.5-14.5, mean around 7.5-8.9, and 96 readings per day with 15-min intervals. The query day has all zero values: min=0, max=0, mean=0, std=0. This is clearly a manipulated day. The pattern is completely flat at zero.

We need to classify as Manipulated, and identify the attack type. The description: "Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration." That exactly matches: the entire day is zero. Attack 2: on-off attack replaces samples with zeros for a random duration, not necessarily the whole day. Attack 5 replaces samples with false values based on average consumption, not zeros. Attack 7 subtracts a cut-off and may zero some but not all. Attack IEEE zeros some intervals but not all. So Attack 0 is most likely. Other attacks: Attack 1 multiplies by constant (0,1), which would preserve some shape but reduced magnitude, not all zeros. Attack 3 multiplies by varying random factors, would not produce all zeros unless factor is zero for all, but that's unlikely. Attack 4 reduces over a period, not all zeros. Attack 6 replaces with cut-off, not zeros. Attack 9 replaces with average, constant not zero. Attack 10 reverses pattern, not zeros. Attack 12 replaces with lower user, not zeros. So Attack 0 is the only one that results in all zeros for the entire day.

Confidence is high because the query day is exactly all zeros, which is the definition of Attack 0. We need to output Classification: Manipulated, and fill the rest.

Attack Behavior Explanation: All consumption samples are replaced with zero values for the entire day. Completely eliminates both trend and magnitude. Possible causes: total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Matches the definition.

Thus output:

Classification: Manipulated
Identified Attack:
- Most Likely: Attack 0
- Confidence: High
- Reasoning: The query day shows all values equal to zero across all 96 intervals, which directly matches Attack 0 (all samples replaced by zeros). Attack 2 (on-off) would zero only a random duration, not necessarily the entire day; Attack IEEE zeroes intermittent intervals, not necessarily all. Both alternatives would leave some non-zero values, which is not observed here.
Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated: All actual energy consumption readings are replaced with 0.0 across the entire day (96 consecutive 15-minute intervals).
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Magnitude is reduced to zero; the consumption trend is completely eliminated (flat line at zero).
- Possible technical causes: Total meter bypass (e.g., physical dislocation of meter, bridging terminals), severe meter malfunction/failure, or false data injection that overwrites all readings with zero.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

So Attack 0 is the best match.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. Attack 7 replaces exceeding values with zero, but that wouldn't result in all zeros unless the cutoff is zero, but it subtracts a cutoff and zeros negative results; if the cutoff is high enough, many samples become zero, but it wouldn't necessarily zero all samples unless the cutoff exceeds all original values. But the original day 4 had values around 37.61 to 111.20, so subtracting a cutoff that high would zero everything? Unlikely. Moreover, Attack 7 description: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption." That would produce zeros only for samples below the cutoff after subtraction? Actually, if you subtract a cutoff X from each sample, samples below X become negative and set to 0, samples above X become positive difference. So to get all zeros, you'd need X > max consumption, then all samples become (value - X) negative -> zero. That could be a specific instance of Attack 7. But Attack 0 explicitly says all zeros. So Attack 0 is more direct.

Given the description, Attack 0 is the one that replaces all with zeros. Confidence high.

Reasoning: The entire day's consumption is zero, which perfectly matches Attack 0's definition of replacing all samples with zero. Attack 2 would only zero a random duration, not the whole day. Attack IEEE would result in some non-zero intervals. Thus, Attack 0 is the most likely.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Output Format:
Classification:
- Original | Manipulated

Identified Attack:  (fill only if manipulated)
- Most Likely: Attack X
- Confidence: High | Medium | Low
- Reasoning: Why it matches Attack X and why Attack Y and Attack Z were ruled out (list top 2 alternatives considered)

- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week
- Impact on magnitude and trend
- Possible technical causes

We are given the reference week data and the query day data. The query day (DAY_ID=3) shows all zero values. The statistics for the query day: min=0.00, max=0.00, std=0.00, mean=0.00. This indicates every 15-minute reading is zero.

The reference week days have non-zero consumption with typical ranges (min around 10-12, max around 37-46, means around 19-24). The query day is a flat zero profile. This is clearly not consistent with any of the reference days. So the day is manipulated.

Now, which attack type? Attack 0 is defined as "Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration." That matches exactly. The entire query day has zero values.

Check other possibilities:
Attack 2: on-off attack, replaces samples with zeros for a random duration, but not necessarily all day. Here it's the entire day, so Attack 0 is more fitting.
Attack 7: subtracts a cut-off point, but if the result is negative, zero is reported. However, that would leave some non-zero readings if any original consumption is below the cut-off. All zeros suggests a uniform zero replacement, not a subtraction with negative flooring.
Attack IEEE: zeroes out some intervals but not all. Here all intervals are zero.
Attack 3,4,5,6,10,12 all would leave some non-zero values or altered values, not a complete zero profile.

Thus, Attack 0 is the most likely.

Reasoning content 
We are given: Reference week has 7 days of normal consumption readings (96 per day, 15-min intervals). Query day is Day_ID=2 (which corresponds to the same weekday as reference Day 2? Actually, reference Day 2 is Tuesday (dow=1) and query day is also dow=1 (Tuesday), but different month and day of month. The query day has all readings 0.00. Mean, min, max, std all zero. So the query day is completely zero for the entire day.

Now, comparing to reference week: The reference week shows typical consumption varies from about 3.8 to 28.48, with daily means around 10-13. The query day is all zeros. This is a clear manipulation. No normal day would have exactly zero consumption for the entire 24-hour period (96 readings). Even at night, there is some base load (min around 3.8-5.06 in reference days). So the classification is "Manipulated".

Now, which attack type does this correspond to? Looking at the definitions:

- Attack 0: "Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration." That exactly matches: all 96 samples are zero. The description says: "Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile." So Attack 0 is the perfect match.

Other possibilities: Attack 2 is on-off attack: "replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration." But here it's the entire day, not random duration. Attack 7: cut-off subtraction might lead to zeros, but Attack 7 subtracts a cut-off, so values above cut-off become cut-off, and below zero become zero. That would not produce all zeros unless the cut-off was zero, and even then not all samples would be zero if any were exactly zero? Actually, if cut-off is zero, subtracting 0 from each sample yields original values, not zeros. So Attack 7 wouldn't yield all zeros. Attack IEEE: some intervals zero, some real, not all zeros. Attack 5 replaces with average times random, not all zero. Attack 1 multiplies by factor (0,1), could produce near zero if factor very small, but the readings are exactly zero, not tiny values. The query day has exactly 0.00 for every reading. That's highly indicative of Attack 0. Attack 0 explicitly says "replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration." So that's it.

Confidence: High, because the pattern is an exact match to the definition. Alternatives considered: Attack 2 (on-off) but ruled out because it's the entire day, not random duration. Attack 1 (multiply by constant) could be extremely small factor leading to rounding to zero, but the readings are exactly 0.00; however, real consumption values in reference are not extremely low (minimum 3.8). Multiplying by a very small factor like 0.001 would give values like 0.0038, which might be recorded as 0.00 if precision is 2 decimals? Given that reference readings have two decimal places (e.g., 14.56, 10.76), they are likely recorded to two decimals. Multiplying by a factor <0.007 would give values less than 0.04, which could round to 0.00 if the meter reports with 2 decimals. But Attack 1 multiplies by a constant random value between (0,1). If the factor is chosen very small, all values could become effectively zero in the reported data. However, the description of Attack 0 specifically says "replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values", which is exactly what we see. Attack 1 mentions "multiples the actual energy consumption by a constant random value" and "the consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced." If the factor is so small that all values become zero, the trend would be flat zero, not the same trend. So Attack 1 is not a perfect match because the trend should remain similar shape but scaled down, not all zeros. With all zeros, there's no trend. So Attack 0 is much more likely.

Thus: Classification: Manipulated. Identified Attack: Attack 0. Confidence: High. Reasoning: All 96 readings are zero, matching Attack 0's definition exactly. Attack 2 (on-off) would have zeros for only part of the day, not the entire day. Attack 1 would produce scaled-down values preserving the trend, not flat zeros.

Attack behavior explanation: Attack 0 replaces all consumption readings with zero, effectively reporting no energy usage for the entire day. This eliminates both magnitude and trend completely. Possible technical causes: total meter bypass (e.g., direct physical tampering to bypass the meter), severe meter malfunction (e.g., meter failure causing zero readings), or false data injection that sets all readings to zero.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Attack 2 (on‑off attack) would show intermittent zero and non‑zero periods, not a full day of zeros. Attack IEEE (15‑minute random zeroing) would also produce a mix of zero and non‑zero values. No other attack type yields an entirely zero day.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- The attacker has replaced every 15‑minute consumption reading for the entire day with 0.00, completely eliminating both the trend (typical morning/evening peaks) and magnitude (all non‑zero values) seen in the normal week.
- This results in a flat zero profile, equivalent to no reported energy usage.
- Possible technical causes: total meter bypass (physical tampering that routes current around the meter), severe meter malfunction, or false data injection that forces all recorded values to zero.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Reasoning content 
We are given reference week data for a smart meter (7 days, 96 readings per day at 15-min intervals). The query day is DAY_ID=4, which has all readings as 0.00. The metadata: CONS_NO=98, MONTH_ID=29, WEEK_ID=3, DAY_ID=4 (Thursday). The reference week shows typical consumption with nighttime lows around 175-200, daytime peaks varying but often around 400-670. The query day is all zeros across the full day. 

Looking at the definitions:
Attack 0: "Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration." That exactly matches: all readings are zero. Attack 0 describes a total meter bypass or severe malfunction, completely eliminating trend and magnitude.

Other attacks:
- Attack 1: multiplies by constant between 0 and 1, would yield non-zero values unless the factor was exactly 0, but the definition says between (0,1) so not exactly zero. The readings are all zero, so it's not Attack 1.
- Attack 2: replacement with zeros for a random duration. Here it's entire day, not random. So Attack 2 would be a subset of intervals, not all.
- Attack 3: multiplying by different random factors, again not all zero unless all factors are 0, which is unlikely.
- Attack 4: reduction over a randomly selected period, not necessarily zero, so not.
- Attack 5: replacing with average normal consumption times random variable between 0 and 1, would produce values not all zero unless random variable always 0, unlikely.
- Attack 6: cut-off value replacement, would produce values at cut-off, not zero unless cut-off is zero? The definition says samples above cut-off are replaced by that cut-off value, so if cut-off is zero, then all replaced by 0, but it says "replacing all consumption values above a random cut-off point by that cut-off value," so it's not all values; values below cut-off remain. Here everything is zero, so even values that would be below cut-off are zero, so not.
- Attack 7: subtract cut-off, if negative then zero. Could potentially yield many zeros if the cut-off is large and most samples go negative. But the query day is entirely zero; with Attack 7, some samples might remain positive if they exceed cut-off. So unlikely to be all zeros.
- Attack 9: all samples replaced by average daily energy consumption, constant but not zero (mean of normal days is around 300-350, so would be flat non-zero). Not zero.
- Attack 10: reversal without reducing total amount, not zero.
- Attack 12: replacing pattern with lower-consuming user, not all zero.
- Attack IEEE: for each 15-min interval, either actual or zero. That could potentially produce all zeros if the attacker chooses to report zero for all intervals, but Attack IEEE is described as "... the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero... across the full day" which might sometimes result in all zeros. However, Attack 0 is specifically defined as replacing all samples with zero for the entire observation duration. Given that the query day is entirely zeros, Attack 0 is the most direct match. Attack IEEE could also produce all zeros, but Attack IEEE implies some intervals may be actual, not necessarily all zero. The description: "For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero." So it could, in theory, result in all zeros if the choice is zero for every interval. But Attack 0 is explicitly "replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration." So Attack 0 is more precise. Attack IEEE is more variable. The confidence should be High.

Now check the normal week: Day 4 of the reference week (DAY_ID=4) is a Thursday, and the query day is also DAY_ID=4 but from a different month (cal_month=4 vs 3) and dom=25. The reference day 4 had min=182.12, max=619.21, mean=348.27. So entirely zeros is grossly inconsistent. It's manipulated.

Thus classification: Manipulated. Identified Attack: Attack 0. Confidence: High. Reasoning: All consumption readings are zero for the entire day, exactly matching the definition of Attack 0. Alternative attacks like Attack IEEE could theoretically produce all zeros, but Attack 0 specifically describes total zero replacement over the entire observation duration. Attack 2 would be partial zeros for random duration, not the full day. Attack 7 could yield zeros for samples below cut-off but would not be all zeros unless the cut-off is extremely high, which would be unlikely given the normal consumption distribution. So Attack 0 is the best fit.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation: For Attack 0, the consumption values are all replaced by zero, completely eliminating the magnitude and trend of the consumption profile. Possible technical causes include total meter bypass (e.g., directly connecting the home to the grid bypassing the meter) or severe meter malfunction (e.g., meter failure where it records nothing).

Output accordingly.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.

1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.

Reasoning content 
We are given reference week data and a query day. The query day is all zeros. The reference week shows consumption varies throughout the day, with min values around 700-900 and max around 1700-1800, with typical patterns. The query day is completely flat with zero consumption for every 15-minute interval. That is highly suspicious. The normal week never had a day with all zeros; the lowest min was 722.22 on Day 1, but it still had variation. So this day is clearly manipulated.

Now, which attack type? Attack 0 is "replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration." That matches perfectly: all values are zero. Attack 2 is on-off attack with zeros for a random duration, but here the entire day is zeros, not a random duration. Attack IEEE is for each interval, either real or zero, creating an irregular pattern, but here it's all zeros, so not that. Attack 7 subtracts a cut-off point; if result negative, zero, but that would not yield uniformly zero unless the cut-off is extremely high, and it would retain some positive values if original consumption > cut-off. Not all zeros. Attack 3,4,5,6, etc. involve scaling or replacing with other values, not uniformly zero. So Attack 0 is the only one that matches all-zero pattern.

Thus, classification: Manipulated. Identified Attack: Attack 0. Confidence: High. Alternatives considered: Attack 2 (on-off) would have some non-zero intervals but here all are zero; Attack IEEE would mix real and zero, not all zeros.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

- Impact on magnitude and trend
- Possible technical causes
Reasoning content 
We are given a reference week of normal consumption data for a smart meter, and a query day (DAY_ID=6) which is the same as Day 6 in the reference week. The reference week shows Day 6 with a typical pattern: values range from around 431 to 1718, with a mean around 1185 and standard deviation around 433. The query day, however, shows all values as 0.00. This is a stark difference: the entire day's consumption is reported as zero.

We are asked to determine if the day is attacked or original. Obviously, the query day is completely zero, which is highly inconsistent with the normal week. The normal Day 6 (which is also Saturday, dow=5) shows a normal consumption profile with morning and evening peaks. So the query day is manipulated.

Now, which attack type does this match? Attack 0 is defined as "Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration." That exactly describes what we see: all samples are zero. Attack 2 is "Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration" but here it's for the entire day, not a random duration within the day. Attack IEEE is similar but it says "the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero", creating an irregular pattern with some zeros and some actual values. Here, all are zero, not a mix. Attack 5 replaces each sample with a false value obtained by multiplying average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, which would produce small non-zero values, not zeros. Attack 6 and 7 involve cut-offs, but would produce non-zero values for some samples. So the most likely is Attack 0.

Thus, classification: Manipulated, Attack 0. Confidence: High. Reasoning: The query day has all zero values, exactly matching Attack 0 definition. Attack 2 (on-off) and Attack IEEE (partial zeroing) were considered but ruled out because they would have non-zero intervals. Attack 5 would produce non-zero values.

Attack behavior: All consumption samples replaced by zero for the entire day. Impact: magnitude and trend completely eliminated. Possible causes: total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction.

Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

- Impact on magnitude and trend

Reasoning content 
We are asked to analyze a query day with all zero consumption values, compared to a reference week showing typical consumption patterns with varying magnitudes and daily trends. The query day has min=0.00, max=0.00, std=0.00, mean=0.00, all readings zero. This clearly indicates manipulation. The normal week data shows non-zero consumption values with typical patterns (higher during daytime, lower at night). The query day being zero throughout suggests a total nullification of consumption reporting. This matches Attack 0 definition: "Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration." It is a total meter bypass or severe malfunction. Other attacks like Attack 2 (on-off attack) would have some non-zero periods; Attack 1 multiplies by constant factor, so trend remains but magnitude reduced, not zero. Attack 5 produces random values from average normal consumption, not zeros. Attack 7 is subtraction with cutoff, resulting in zero only for values below cutoff, not all zeros. So Attack 0 is the most likely. We'll rule out Attack 2 and Attack 5 as alternatives. Confidence high because the day is entirely zero. Output as per format.

Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

- Original | Manipulated
