Attack_Behavior_Explanation:  (fill only if manipulated)
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week
- Impact on magnitude and trend
- Possible technical causes
Reasoning content 
We are given a reference week (7 days) of energy consumption for a smart meter, and a query day (DAY_ID=6) which has all zero values. The reference week shows typical consumption patterns with non-zero values, typical min/max/std/mean. The query day is entirely zero. Let's analyze.

First, we need to assess whether the day is original or manipulated. The reference week shows that the consumer has non-zero consumption throughout each day, with daily means ranging from ~502 to ~565, and a typical pattern with a peak in the evening and lower usage at night. The query day is completely zero, with min=0, max=0, mean=0. This is a drastic departure from the normal pattern. It is not consistent with typical behavior. Clearly manipulated.

Attack types:
Attack 0: All samples replaced with zero values for entire observation duration. That matches exactly: the query day has all zeros. So Attack 0 is the most likely.

We should rule out other attacks:
Attack 2: On-off attack, zeros for random duration. The query day has zeros for the entire day, not just random duration. So not Attack 2.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-min interval, either real consumption or zero. The query day is all zeros, no mixtures. So not IEEE.
Attack 7: Cut-off point subtracted, negative becomes zero. That would result in some non-zero values if the cut-off is less than the actual consumption. Here everything is zero, so not.
Attack 5: Multiplying average by random variable between 0 and 1. That would produce non-zero constant-like values but not all zeros unless the random variable is exactly zero (unlikely for all samples). So ruled out.
Attack 1: Multiplying by constant factor between 0 and 1. If factor is zero, then all zeros, but that's essentially Attack 0. Attack 1 typically uses a factor between 0 and 1 exclusive? But it says "constant random value between (0,1)". So factor cannot be 0. Even if factor is very small, values would be non-zero. Here all are exactly zero. So Attack 1 ruled out.
Attack 3: Different factors per sample, between 0 and 1, would yield non-zero tiny values. Not all zero.
Attack 4: Reduction over a period, still non-zero overall unless reduction to exactly zero for that period, but the entire day zero suggests full period zero.
Attack 6: Cut-off value replacement, would give capped values above a threshold, but still non-zero below threshold. Here all zero.
Attack 10: Pattern reversal, no reduction in total consumption, so non-zero.
Attack 12: Pattern replacement with lower-consuming user, still non-zero.
Attack 9: All samples replaced by average daily energy consumption, resulting in constant flat profile but non-zero.

So Attack 0 is the only one that produces all zero values for the entire day.

Thus answer:
Classification: Manipulated
Identified Attack: Most Likely: Attack 0
Confidence: High
Reasoning: The query day consists entirely of zero readings. The reference week shows consistent non-zero consumption with typical patterns. This is a complete elimination of consumption data, exactly matching Attack 0 (replace all samples with zero). Attack 2 (on-off) would have zeros only for random durations, but the entire day is zero. Attack IEEE would mix real and zero values, not all zeros. Thus Attack 0 is the clear choice.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated: Every 15-minute consumption reading in the day is replaced by 0.0, effectively reporting no energy usage.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Both magnitude and trend are completely eliminated. The profile becomes a flat line at zero.
- Possible technical causes: Total meter bypass (physical bypass causing no measurement), severe meter malfunction, or false data injection sending zero values for the entire day.

Now produce output in requested format.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.

Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

- Original | Manipulated

- Confidence: High | Medium | Low
- Reasoning: Why it matches Attack X and why Attack Y and Attack Z were ruled out (list top 2 alternatives considered)

- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week
- Impact on magnitude and trend
- Possible technical causes
Reasoning content 
We are given data for a reference week and a query day. The query day has all zero values for every 15-minute interval. The reference week shows typical consumption with values ranging from around 285 to 702, with daily means around 470-523. The query day has min=0, max=0, std=0, mean=0. This is clearly not normal behavior. The consumption pattern is entirely zero, which matches Attack 0: "Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration."

We need to classify it as manipulated, identify the attack type, and provide explanation.

Attack 0 fits perfectly: all zero values for the entire day. No other attack type produces a continuous zero pattern; Attack 2 is on-off attack with zeros for random duration, but here it's the entire day, so it's Attack 0. Attack 7 also involves zeros but replaces values exceeding a cut-off with zero, not all zeros. Attack IEEE involves some zeros and some real readings, not all zeros. So Attack 0 is the most likely.

Confidence: High, because the pattern is unambiguous.

Alternatives ruled out: Attack 2 (on-off attack) would have some non-zero intervals; Attack 7 would have some non-zero values unless the cut-off is extremely low, but even then it would only zero out above a threshold, not all values.

Behavior: Attack 0 replaces all samples with zero, completely eliminating magnitude and trend. Possible causes: total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction.

Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.

Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.

Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. Reasoning content 
We are given a smart meter's reference week of 7 days (Day 0 to Day 6) and a query day (DAY_ID=4, which in the reference week corresponds to Day 4, a Thursday). The metadata says: CONS_NO=189, MONTH_ID=28, WEEK_ID=2, DAY_ID=4, Season=Spring, Weekday=Thursday, Date=2014-03-13. But in the per-day temporal features, it says for Query day (DAY_ID=4): cal_month=3, dow=3, dom=18. Since dow=3 means Wednesday? Wait: dow=0..6 Mon-Sun. So dow=3 is Thursday? Let's check: 0=Mon, 1=Tue, 2=Wed, 3=Thu, 4=Fri, 5=Sat, 6=Sun. So dow=3 is Thursday. That matches the Weekday=Thursday. But the dom (day of month) is 18, and month is 3 (March). Date=2014-03-13? That would be March 13, 2014, which is a Thursday. The dom=18 is inconsistent: if month=3 and dom=18, that would be March 18, but the date says March 13. Possibly a discrepancy in metadata, but that's not critical.

The query day readings: All 96 intervals are exactly 0.00. The per-day statistics: min=0.00, max=0.00, std=0.00, mean=0.00. This is a clear zero consumption day.

The reference week shows typical consumption patterns with night-time lows around 2300-2500, rising in the morning, with peaks up to 6600 or so. Day 4 in the reference week (the same day of week, Thursday) has min=2368.42, max=6463.68, mean=4907.35. So a normal Thursday has significant consumption.

Comparing, the query day is all zeros. This is a complete elimination of consumption. According to the attack definitions:

Attack 0: "Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile."

Attack 2: "Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack." That would have some zeros but not all day, unless the random duration happens to be the entire day. But Attack 2 is random duration, so it could theoretically cover the whole day, but attack 0 is specifically defined as all zeros for the entire observation. Given the entire day is zero, Attack 0 is the most precise match. Attack 2 is characterized by "random duration", not necessarily the whole day.

Attack 7: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported." That would result in zeros for some low values, but not all zeros unless the cut-off is extremely high. But if the cut-off is above all normal consumption, all values become zero? Actually, if you subtract a cut-off point from each sample, and if the cut-off is greater than max consumption, each sample becomes negative, so reported as zero. That would also yield all zeros. However, Attack 7 says "Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point." Attack 6: "A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value." Attack 7: replaces exceeding values with zero. That implies for samples above cut-off, they are set to zero? Wait, the description: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported." So it's not just above cut-off; it's subtracting the cut-off from all samples, so those below the cut-off become negative and zero. So if cut-off is very high (above max), all become zero. But is that the intended attack? Attack 7 is described as "Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point." That's ambiguous: Attack 6 replaces above-cut-off values with cut-off; Attack 7 replaces above-cut-off values with zero. But then it says "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported." That is a different mechanism: subtract cut-off, floor at zero. That results in reduced values for those above cut-off (by the cut-off amount) and zeros for those below. But if the cut-off is set high enough, it can zero out all samples. However, typical Attack 7 would not aim for all zeros; it would select a cut-off that only partially reduces consumption. But if the attacker sets cut-off = max consumption, then all samples become zero (since max - max = 0 for max, others negative -> zero). So it's possible but less direct.

Attack 0 explicitly says "all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration." That exactly matches. Attack 8 is not listed. Attack 12? No. Attack 9 is constant average. Attack 5 is random false values. Attack IEEE is partial zeroing across intervals, not all.

Thus, the most likely attack is Attack 0. Confidence: High. The reasoning: All 96 readings are zero, matching Attack 0's complete zeroing. Alternative attacks: Attack 2 if the random duration happened to be the entire day, but Attack 0 is a more specific match; Attack 7 if the cut-off was set extremely high, but Attack 0 is explicit. So we'll rule out others.

Now, output format:

Classification: Manipulated
Identified Attack: Attack 0
Confidence: High
Reasoning: Why it matches Attack 0, and why Attack 2 and Attack 7 were ruled out (list top 2 alternatives considered). Attack 2 is on-off attack but typically partial; Attack 7 could result in all zeros if cut-off is > max, but Attack 0 is more straightforward.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation: 
- How: All consumption samples replaced with zero.
- Impact: Completely eliminates trend and magnitude.
- Possible technical causes: Total meter bypass, severe meter malfunction.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

- Original | Manipulated

Identified Attack:  (fill only if manipulated)
- Most Likely: Attack X
- Confidence: High | Medium | Low
- Reasoning: Why it matches Attack X and why Attack Y and Attack Z were ruled out (list top 2 alternatives considered)

- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week
- Impact on magnitude and trend
- Possible technical causes
Reasoning content 
We are given a reference week (7 days) of 15-minute readings for a smart meter, and a query day (DAY_ID=5) which is also the same weekday as Day 5 in the reference week (Friday). The query day has all zero values. The reference week shows typical consumption patterns: all days have non-zero values, with typical daily min around 50-60, max up to 165, mean around 100-107, and a daily pattern with low night-time consumption and higher daytime peaks. The query day is entirely zero across all 96 intervals. This is a classic case of Attack 0: "Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration." It completely eliminates both trend and magnitude.

No other attack fits: Attack 1 multiplies by a constant random factor, which would preserve some positive values. Attack 2 replaces with zeros for a random duration, not the whole day. Attack 3 multiplies by varying factors, still positive. Attack 4 reduces only a period. Attack 5 replaces with false average-based values, which would be non-zero unless the average is zero, which it's not. Attack 6 caps values, would produce non-zero values unless the cutoff is zero, but typical cutoffs would be above zero. Attack 7 subtracts a cutoff, but would still leave some positive values unless cutoff is high, but here all are zero. Attack 9 replaces with average, yielding a constant non-zero. Attack 10 reverses pattern, non-zero. Attack 12 replaces with another user's pattern, non-zero. Attack IEEE zeroes out some intervals, not all. So it's definitely Attack 0.

Confidence: High. Reasoning: all values are exactly zero, which matches Attack 0 definition perfectly. The reference week shows normal non-zero consumption, ruling out a permanent zero consumption pattern. So the day is manipulated.

Identified Attack:
- Most Likely: Attack 0
- Confidence: High
- Reasoning: The query day contains 96 consecutive readings of 0.00, which exactly matches Attack 0’s definition of replacing all samples with zero for the entire observation duration. This results in a completely flat, zero consumption profile. Attack 2 (on‑off) zeroes only random durations, not the full day. Attack 7 subtracts a cut‑off and zeroes negative results, but to achieve all zeros the cut‑off would have to exceed the maximum possible consumption; Attack 0 is the direct mapping for a full‑day zero event.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated: Every 15‑minute reading for the day is set to zero, regardless of the true consumption. Compared to the reference week, where daily means ranged from ~530 to ~575 with peaks up to ~800, no energy use is reported at all.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Both magnitude and trend are entirely eliminated; the consumption profile is a constant zero, with no diurnal pattern or load variation.
- Possible technical causes: Complete meter bypass (e.g., illegal direct connection to the supply), severe meter malfunction, or a targeted false data injection that overwrites all recorded values with zero to conceal actual consumption.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

- Confidence: High | Medium | Low
- Reasoning: Why it matches Attack X and why Attack Y and Attack Z were ruled out (list top 2 alternatives considered)

We are given a reference week of 7 days (Day 0 to Day 6) each with 96 readings (15-minute intervals) and a query day (DAY_ID=5) with 96 readings all 0.00. The query day's statistics: min=0.00, max=0.00, std=0.00, mean=0.00. The reference week days have typical consumption patterns with peaks around 700-800, means around 530-575, and minimums around 270-300. The query day is completely flat at zero.

This is clearly a manipulated day. The pattern is all zeros. This matches Attack 0 exactly: "Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration." It eliminates both trend and magnitude. Other attacks involve partial zeros (Attack 2, on-off) or scaling (Attacks 1,3,4, etc.), but here it's a full day of zeros. Attack IEEE also zeros some intervals but not all. Attack 7 subtracts a cut-off and zeros negative values, but the result wouldn't be all zeros unless the cut-off is extremely high, and even then it might leave some non-zero if original values are above cut-off? But Attack 7 says: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported." If the cut-off is very high, all readings become zero. But Attack 0 explicitly says all samples replaced with zero, which is exactly what we see. Attack 7 would require that the cut-off be greater than the maximum consumption value in the day to produce all zeros, which is possible but less likely than a direct zeroing. Attack 2 zeros "for a random duration" not the entire day. Attack 0 is designed for total zeroing.

Thus, the most likely attack is Attack 0. Confidence high because the data is all zeros.

Alternatives considered: Attack 2 (on-off) but it is continuous zeros for full day, not partial. Attack 7 could theoretically produce zeros if cut-off is high enough, but it's not the primary definition; Attack 0 is more specific.

Identified Attack:
- Most Likely: Attack 0
- Confidence: High
- Reasoning: The query day consists entirely of zero readings for all 96 intervals, matching the definition of Attack 0 (all samples replaced by zeros). The reference Friday (Day 6) shows a normal consumption pattern with values ranging from ~108 to ~357. The query day deviates completely, with no non-zero values. Attack 2 (on-off attack) was considered but ruled out because it zeroes only a random portion of the day, not the entire 24-hour period. Attack IEEE was also considered, but it replaces only some intervals with zero, not all. The full-day zero profile uniquely points to Attack 0.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated: Every 15-minute reading for the entire observation day is set to exactly 0.0, regardless of the actual energy used. Instead of reflecting the typical daily consumption pattern seen in the reference week (e.g., morning ramp-up, daytime peaks, evening decline), all values are uniformly zero.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: The magnitude is reduced to zero; the entire consumption profile becomes a flat line. All temporal trends (morning/evening peaks, overnight baseload) are eliminated. The attack completely erases any evidence of energy usage.
- Possible technical causes: Total meter bypass (e.g., the meter is physically circumvented so no current is measured), severe meter malfunction (meter stopped registering), or a false data injection that continuously reports zero readings.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Output Format:
Classification:
- Original | Manipulated

Identified Attack:  (fill only if manipulated)
- Most Likely: Attack X
- Confidence: High | Medium | Low
- Reasoning: Why it matches Attack X and why Attack Y and Attack Z were ruled out (list top 2 alternatives considered)

Reasoning content 
We are given a reference week of consumption data for a smart meter (CONS_NO=215, WEEK_ID=1, DAY_ID=6 for the query day). The query day (DAY_ID=6) is a Saturday (dow=5) in January (cal_month=1). The reference week includes days 0 through 6, which are labeled as: Day 0: Saturday (dow=6), Day 1: Sunday (dow=0), Day 2: Monday (dow=1), Day 3: Tuesday (dow=2), Day 4: Wednesday (dow=3), Day 5: Thursday (dow=4), Day 6: Friday (dow=5). So the query day is also a Friday (dow=5) but with dom=13 instead of dom=6. So it's a different Friday.

The reference week's Day 6 (Friday) has min=108.41, max=357.32, std=73.85, mean=242.70. The query day has all values 0.00, so min=0, max=0, std=0, mean=0. Clearly this is completely zero for the entire day.

This matches the description of Attack 0: "Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile."

The normal Friday (Day 6 in reference) shows a typical consumption pattern with morning increase (around interval 18: 170.10, rising to a peak of 357.32 at interval 60, then dropping). The query day is flat zero. So it's clearly manipulated.

We must rule out other attacks that could also produce zeros, like Attack 2 (on-off attack with zeros for a random duration) but here the entire day is zero, not just a random duration. Attack 7 might produce zeros if the cut-off point is high, but it says "replaces exceeding values with zero" meaning only values above the cut-off become zero, not all. Attack IEEE randomly zeroes some intervals. So Attack 0 is the only one that replaces all samples with zero for the entire observation.

Thus, classification: Manipulated. Identified Attack: Attack 0. Confidence: High.

Attack behavior explanation: All consumption values are replaced with zero, eliminating magnitude and trend entirely. Possible causes: total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction.

We'll list top alternatives: Attack 2 and Attack IEEE, but ruled out because they only produce partial zeros.

Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Reasoning content 
We are asked to classify a query day (DAY_ID=5) as Original or Manipulated based on a reference week of normal consumption for the same meter. The query day readings are all 0.00. The reference week shows clear daily patterns with moderate consumption at night and higher peaks during day/evening. The statistics: query day min=0, max=0, std=0, mean=0. This is clearly anomalous. The normal week shows days with means around 148-160, mins around 81-90, max around 190-225. A day of all zeros is a drastic change. It is not consistent with normal behavior. So it is Manipulated.

Attack type: All zeros for the entire day matches Attack 0 description exactly: "Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration." That is a total meter bypass or severe malfunction. No other attack has this pattern: Attack 2 is zeros for random durations, not whole day; Attack 5 replaces with random values around average; Attack 7 subtracts cut-off but doesn't produce all zeros unless cut-off is above all values? Actually Attack 7: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported." Could produce all zeros if the cut-off is higher than all normal values, but it's unlikely to be that high because normal max is around 224, cut-off would need to be above that, but then the resulting values would be zero for all. However, Attack 7 description says "Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption." In Attack 7, only values above the cut-off are zeroed? Wait, let's read: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported." So it subtracts a constant from every sample, and any that go negative become zero. So if the constant is high enough (e.g., 300), all samples become zero. That could produce all zeros. But Attack 0 specifically says "replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration." That is exactly what we observe. Given the description, Attack 0 is the most straightforward match. Attack 7 is not intended to produce all zeros; it's a cut-off subtraction that typically leaves some positive values. But if the attacker chooses a very high cut-off, it could yield all zeros, but that would be a degenerate case of Attack 7. However, Attack 0 is explicitly defined for this scenario. The behavior of Attack 0: completely eliminates both trend and magnitude. Causes: total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Given the query day is entirely zero, it's overwhelmingly likely Attack 0. So we'll classify as Manipulated, most likely Attack 0 with high confidence.

We must also list top 2 alternatives considered and rule them out. Consider Attack 2: could be on-off attack with zeros for random duration. But here it's the whole day, not random durations. So it's not Attack 2. Consider Attack 7: could theoretically produce all zeros if cut-off > max consumption. But Attack 7's typical behavior is not all zeros, and the description says "Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption." Here trend is completely flat, so it changes trend. Actually Attack 7 says "Does not change the trend" but subtracting a constant from all values preserves trend shape (ups and downs) but if many become zero, trend might be flattened for those zeroed periods. But if all are zero, trend is flat, so it's not consistent with "does not change the trend". Attack 0 explicitly eliminates both trend and magnitude. So Attack 0 is perfect.

Thus output:

Classification: Manipulated
Identified Attack: Most Likely: Attack 0, Confidence: High
Reasoning: All 96 readings are zero, matching Attack 0's definition of replacing all samples with zero. Attack 2 (random zero durations) is ruled out because zeroing is not random but uniform across entire day. Attack 7 (subtract cut-off) could yield all zeros only if cut-off exceeds maximum normal consumption (~224), but that would be an extreme and unlikely choice, and Attack 7 is characterized by preserving trend, whereas here trend is eliminated. Attack 0 is the only attack that consistently and entirely zeroes consumption for the whole observation period.
Attack_Behavior_Explanation: The consumption values are all set to zero, completely eliminating any signal. Magnitude reduced to zero; trend is flat. This simulates a total meter bypass (e.g., physical tampering to prevent measurement) or severe meter malfunction/failure.

Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.

- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: Every 15‑minute consumption sample for the day is forced to 0.00, completely erasing the typical daily pattern seen in the reference week (which shows morning rises, midday peaks, and evening declines with values in the range ~50–160).
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Both magnitude and trend are completely eliminated; the day appears as a flat zero line with no variation, unlike the reference days that have clear load shapes.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

- Reasoning: Why it matches Attack X and why Attack Y and Attack Z were ruled out (list top 2 alternatives considered)

We are asked to determine if the query day is manipulated, and if so, which attack type. The query day has all zero readings (96 zeros). The reference week shows typical non-zero consumption patterns for each day: they have morning, afternoon peaks, and nighttime lows, with typical min around 200-300 and max around 500-700. So a day with all zeros is clearly anomalous.

Other attacks like Attack 2 (on-off) would have zeros for a random duration, but here it's the entire day. We are asked to determine if the query day is manipulated or original, and if manipulated, identify the most likely attack type and explain its behavior.

We have a reference week of 7 days (Day 0 to Day 6) with 96 readings per day (15-minute intervals). The query day is given as DAY_ID=2 (which corresponds to the same day of the week? Actually, metadata says DAY_ID=2 for the query day, and in the reference week, Day 2 is also labeled DAY_ID=2. But the query day has all zero values: min=0, max=0, std=0, mean=0. The per-day statistics for the reference week show typical consumption with means around 678-777, min around 303-349, max around 936-1085. The query day is completely flat at zero.

This is clearly manipulated. The normal week shows significant consumption throughout the day, with typical daily patterns: lower at night, rising in the morning, peaks in the evening. The query day is all zeros. This is not consistent with normal behavior. So classification: Manipulated.

Now, which attack type? Attack 0 is defined as "Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration." That exactly matches: every sample is 0.00. Attack 2 is "replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration" but that would only be partial zeros, not all zeros. Attack IEEE might involve some zeros and some actual values, but here all are zero. So Attack 0 is the most likely.

We must also consider if any other attack could produce all zeros. Attack 7: "A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported." But if the cutoff point is extremely high, all samples could become negative and thus zero. However, Attack 7 would normally leave some values non-zero if the cutoff is not high enough to zero everything. The description says "Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point." It does not typically result in all zeros unless the cutoff is chosen to be above the maximum consumption for the entire day. But the attack is defined as a random cut-off point, so it's less likely to be exactly the entire day zeroed out. Attack 0 specifically matches the all-zero scenario perfectly.

So, most likely Attack 0.

Confidence: High, because the data is entirely zero, matching exactly the definition of Attack 0.

Now, explanation of attack behavior:
- How consumption values are manipulated: All samples are replaced with zero, simulating no energy usage reported.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Magnitude completely eliminated (all zero), trend completely eliminated (flat profile).
- Possible technical causes: Total meter bypass, severe meter malfunction, or false data injection that substitutes zero for all readings.

Other attacks ruled out: Attack 2 would have only a partial duration of zeros, not the whole day. Attack 5 would produce random values based on average consumption multiplied by random factors, not zeros. Attack 9 would give a constant value equal to the average, not zero.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

- Reasoning: Why it matches Attack X and why Attack Y and Attack Z were ruled out (list top 2 alternatives considered)

Attack 5 would produce random values, not all zeros. Attack 1 multiplies by a factor, so if factor is 0 it could be all zeros, but Attack 1 description says multiplies by constant random value between 0 and 1, but does not specifically say it can be exactly 0? Possibly if the random value is 0, it would be all zeros, but Attack 0 explicitly describes "replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values". Given the all-zero profile, Attack 0 is the most direct match. And the description says simulates a total meter bypass or severe malfunction. So we'll go with Attack 0.

But the attack description says: "Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point." That suggests it's like Attack 6 but instead of capping at the cut-off, it shifts by subtracting the cut-off and floors at zero. If you subtract the cut-off from all values, the pattern shape might be preserved for values above cut-off, but reduced. But if the cut-off is high, all become zero. That is possible but less likely than a straightforward all-zero attack. Attack 0 is exactly all zeros. Attack 2 could be on-off for the entire day? But Attack 2: "Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration." So it could be the entire day if the random duration happens to be the whole day. But that's also possible. However, Attack 0 is explicitly "for the entire observation duration." So it's a perfect match. Confidence high.

Also, note that Attack 9 replaces with average daily consumption, not zero. Attack 10 reverses, not zero. Attack 12 replaces with lower-consuming user, not zero. So the all-zero pattern strongly suggests Attack 0. We'll rule out Attack 2 and Attack 7 as top alternatives because they could also yield all zeros but are not the primary definition. Attack 7 would require a cut-off so high that all values become negative, which is unlikely to be chosen randomly. Attack 2's random duration could cover the whole day, but Attack 0 is the canonical full-day zero attack.

Thus the most likely attack is Attack 0.

Confidence: High, because the pattern is unmistakable: a full day with no usage reported while normal week shows regular consumption.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

- Impact on magnitude and trend
